Each\nmini-lab i s o u t f i t t e d t o meet a s p e c i f i c d i s c i p l i n e : blood,\nc a r d i o v a s c u l a r , m e t a b o l i c s t u d i e s , microbiology, n u t r i t i o n\nand endocrinology, and o p e r a t i o n a l medicine.\n\nFood Compatibility Assessment: This investigation will measure\nwhole body metabolic gains or losses, together with associated\nendo~rinoio~ical electrolytes controls. The purpose of\nand\nthe investigation is to assess food compatibility and to determine the effect of space flight upon overall body composition\nand upon the circulating and excretory levels of certain\nhormonal constituents which are responsible for maintaining\nhomeostasis. This assessment is designed to acquire input\nand output information necessary not only to assess the\nmetabolic consequences\'of the final lunar mission but also\nto provide a firmer basis for interpreting the results of the\nSkylab missions.\nAs part of this investigation, an improved urine collection system will be used by the Apollo 17 crew. Called the\nBiomedical Urine Sample System [BUSS), the device consists of\na polyurethane film bag with 4,000 rn~lllliters a 0 ounces1\n2\ncapacity which contains 30 milligrams C.0012 ounces) of\n.\nlithium for a tracer and 10 grams C4 ounces) boric acid\nfor preservation of organic constituents. One bag is furnished\nfor each man/day in the command module.for a total of 34.\nEach bag is fitted with a roll-on cuff.\nAt the end of each 24-hour sampling period, a small\nsample for postflight analysis is withdrawn from each of\nthe three BUSS bags and labeled and stowed for postflight\nanalysis; the remainder is vented overboard through the\ncommand module waste management system. No samples will\nbe collected in lunar module operations.\n\nENGINEERING/OPERATIONAL,\nTESTS\' AND\' DEMONSTRATIONS\n\nHeat Flow and Convection\nThe flow of fluids on Earth are dependent upon gravity\nfor motion, but in the absence of gravity in space flight\nfluids behave quite differently and depend more upon surface\ntension as their motive force, Investigations of fluid flow\ncaused by surface tension gradients, interfacial tension of\ndissimilar fluids and expansion are next to impossible in\nearthbound laboratories.\n\n~\n\nThe Apollo 17 heat flow and convection demonstration\nwill go beyond the demonstration carried out on Apollo 14\nby providing more exact data on the behavior of fluids in\na low gravity field -- data which will be valuable in the\ndesign of future science experiments and for manufacturing\nprocesses in space.\nThree test cells are used in the Apollo 17 demonstration\nfor measuring and observing fluid flow behavior. The tests\nwill be recorded on motion picture film in addition to direct\nobservation by the crew.\nRadial heat flow will be induces in a circular cell\nwhich has an electrical heater in its center. A liquid\ncrystal material, which changes colors when heated, covers\nthe argon-filled cell, thereby indi\'cating heat flow through\nchanging color patterns.\nLineal heat flow will be demonstrated hn a transparent\ncylinder filled with Krytox (heavy oil) and color-indicating\nliquid crystal strips. Heat flow is induced by an electric\nheater in one end of the cylinder.\nThe third device is a flow pattern test cell made up\nof a shallow aluminum dish into which layers of Krytox with\nsuspended aluminum flakes are injected. A heater on the\nbottom of the dish causes flow patterns to form in the Krytox,\nwith the aluminum flakes serving to make the flow more visible.\nThe demonstrations will be run first during translunar\ncoast when khe spacecraft rates are nulled in all three axes,\nand again after passive thermal control (PTC) or "barbe~ue\'~\nroll has been set up, The 16-nun data acquisition camera\nwill be used to make sequence photos of the test cells -- 10\nminutes for the radial and lineal test cells, and 15 minutes\nfor the flow pattern test cell,\n\nSkylab Contamination Study: Since John Glenn reported\nseeing "fireflies" outside the tiny window of his Mercury\nspacecraft Friendship 7 a decade ago, space crews have noted\nlight-scattering particles that hinder visua1,observations\nas well as photographic tasks. ~heseclouds of particles\nsurrounding spacecraft generally are from water dumps and\nescaping cabin gases changing into ice crystals.\nThe phenomenon could be of concern in the Skylab missions\nduring operation of the solar astronomy experiments. The\nlight scattering from a 100-micron particle 13 kilometers (7.8 miles)\naway from the spacecraft, for example, is as bright as a\nthird-magnitude star. A cloud of particles with such a lightscattering effect would rule out any astronomical experiments\nbeing conducted on the sunlit portion of an orbit.\nDuring translunar coast the Apollo 17 Infrared Scanning\nRadiometer and Far-Ultraviolet Spectrometer in the SIM Bay\nwill provide data on optical contamination, adsorption of\nthe contamination cloud, the scattering effect of a particle\ncloud, and the duration of a cloud resulting from a specific\nwaste water dump.\nLight-scattering and contamination data gathered on Apollo\n17 will aid in predicting contamination in the vicinity of\nthe Skylab orbital workshop and its sensitive telescope mount,\nand in devising means of minimizing contaminate levels around\nthe Skylab vehicle. Photography in support of the study\nwas also conducted on Apollo 16.\n\nLUNAR ROVING VEHICLE\n\nThe l u n a r r o v i n g v e h i c l e ( L R V ) , t h e t h i r d t o be used\non t h e Moon, w i l l t r a n s p o r t two a s t r o n a u t s on t h r e e e x p l o r a t i o n t r a v e r s e s of t h e Moon\'s Taurus-Littrow r e g i o n d u r i n g\nt h e Apollo 17 mission. The LRV w i l l a l s o c a r r y t o o l s , s c i e n t i f i c and communications equipment, and l u n a r samples.\nThe f o u r - wheel, l i g h t w e i g h t v e h i c l e h a s g r e a t l y extended t h e l u n a r a r e a t h a t can be e x p l o r e d by man. I t i s t h e\nf i r s t manned s u r f a c e t r a n s p o r t a t i o n system designed t o opera t e on t h e Moon, and it r e p r e s e n t s a s o l u t i o n t o c h a l l e n g i n g\nnew problems w i t h o u t p r e c e d e n t i n Earth-bound v e h i c l e d e s i g n\nand o p e r a t i o n .\nThe LRV must be f o l d e d i n t o a s m a l l package w i t h i n a\nwedge-shaped s t o r a g e bay of t h e l u n a r module d e s c e n t s t a g e\nf o r t r a n s p o r t t o t h e Moon. A f t e r l a n d i n g , t h e v e h i c l e must\non\nbe unfolded from i t s stowed p o s i t i o n am%-..deployed t h e s u r f a c e . I t must t h e n o p e r a t e i n an almost t o t a l vacuum under\nextremes o f s u r f a c e t e m p e r a t u r e s , low g r a v i t y , and on unfamili a r terrain.\nThe f i r s t l u n a r r o v i n g v e h i c l e , used on . t h e Apollo 1 5\nl u n a r m i s s i o n , was d r i v e n f o r t h r e e hours d u r i n g i t s e x p l o r a t i o n t r a v e r s e s , c o v e r i n g a d i s t a n c e of 27.9 k i l o m e t e r s\n(17.3 s t a t u t e m i l e s ) a t an average speed of 9.3 k i l o m e t e r s\nan hour (5.8 m i l e s an hour)\nThe second l u n a r r o v i n g v e h i c l e , used on Apollo 1 6 , was\nd r i v e n t h r e e hours and twenty-six minutes f o r a t o t a l d i s t a n c e\nof 26.9 k i l o m e t e r s (16-7 s t a t u t e m i l e s ) a t an average speed\nof 9.8 k i l o m e t e r s an hour (4.9 m i l e s an h o u r ) ,\nGeneral D e s c r i p t i o n\n\n-\n\nThe LRV i s 3 -1. meters l o n g (10.2 f e e t ) ; h a s a 1 . 8\nm e t e r ( s i x - f o o t ) width; i s 1 . 1 4 meters high (44.8 i n c h e s ) ;\nand h a s a 2-3-meter wheel b a s e (7.5 f e e t ) . Each wheel i s\npowered by a s m a l l e l e c t r i c motor. The maximum speed reached\non t h e Apollo 1 5 mission was 1 3 km/hr ( e i g h t mph), and 17 km/hr\n( Ilmph) on Apollo 1 6 Two 36-volt b a t t e r i e s p r o v i d e v e h i c l e power, and e i t h e r\nb a t t e r y can run a l l systems. The f r o n t and r e a r wheels have\ns e p a r a t e s t e e r i n g systems; i f one f a i l s it can be d i s c o n n e c t e d\nand t h e L V w i l l o p e r a t e w i t h t h e o t h e r system.\nR\n\nWeighing about 209 kilograms (461 pounds),Earth weight,\nwhen deployed on the Moon the LRV can carry a total payl~ad\nof about 490 kilograms (1,080 pounds), more than twice its\nown weight. The payload includes two astronauts and their\nportable life support systems (about 363 kilograms; 800 pounds),\n68.0 kilograms (150 pounds)of communications equipment, 54.5\nkilograms (150 pounds) of scientific equipment and photographic gear, and 40.8 kilograms (90 pounds) of lunar samples.\nThe LRV is designed to operated during a minimum period\nof 78 hours on the lunar surface. It can make several exploration sorties to a cumulative distance of 96 kilometers (57 miles).\nThe maximum distance the LRV will be permitted to range from\nthe lunar module will be approximately 9.4 kilometers (5.9 miles),\nthe distance the crew could safely walk back to the LM in the\nunlikely event of a total LRV failure. This walkback distance\nlimitation is based upon the quantity of oxygen and coolant\navailable in the astronauts\' portable life support systems.\nThis area contains about 292 square kilometers (113 square miles)\navailable for investigation, 10 times the area that can be\nexplored on foot.\nThe vehicle can negotiate obstacles 30.5 centimeters\n(one foot) high and cross crevasses 70 centimeters (28 inches).\nThe fully loaded vehicle can climb and descend slopes as steep\nas 25 degrees, and park on slopes up to 35 degrees. Pitch and\nroll stability angles are at least 45 degrees, and the turn\nradius is three meters (10 feet)\n\n.\n\nBoth crewmen sit so the front wheels are visible during\nnormal driving, The driver uses an on-board dead reckoning\nnavigation system to determine direction and distance from the\nlunar module, and total distance traveled at any point during\na traverse.\nThe LRV has five major systems: mobility, crew station,\nnavigation, power, and thermal control. Secondary systems\ninclude the deployment mechanism, LM attachment equipment, and\nground support equipment.\nThe aluminum chassis is divided into three sections that\nsupport all equipment and systems, The fomard and aft sections\nfold over the center one for stowage in the LM. The forward\nsection holds both batteries, part of the navigation system,\nand electronics gear for the traction drive and steering systems.\nThe center section holds the crew station with its two seats,\ncontrol and display console, and hand controller. The floor\nof beaded aluminum panels can support the weight of both astronauts standing in lunar gravity, The aft section holds the\nscientific payload.\n\nAuxiliary LRV equipment includes the lunar communications relay unit ILCRU) and its high and low gain antennas\nfor direct communications with Earth, the ground commanded\ntelevision assembly (GCTA), scientific equipment, tools,\nand sample stowage bags.\nMobility System\nThe mobility system is the major LRV system, containing\nthe wheels, traction drive, suspension, steering, and drive\ncontrol electronics subsystems.\nThe vehicle is driven by a T-shaped hand controller located on the control and display console post between the\ncrewmen. Using the controller, the astronaut maneuvers the\nLRV forward, reverse, left and right.\nEach LRV wheel has a spun aluminum hub and a titanium\nbump stop (inner frame) inside the tire (outer frame). The\ntire is made of a woven mesh of zinc-coated piano wire to\nwhich titanium treads are riveted in a chevron pattern around\nthe outer circumference. The bump stop prevents excessive\ninflection of the mesh tire during heavy impact. Each wheel\nweighs 5.4 kilograms (12 pounds) on Earth and is designed\nto be driven at least 180 kilometers (112 miles). The wheels\nare 81.3 centimeters (32 inches) in diameter and 22.9 centimeters (nine inches) wide.\nA traction drive attached to each wheel has a motor\nharmonic drive gear unit, and a brake assembly. The harmonic\ndrive reduces motor speed at an 80-to-1 rate for continuous\noperation at all speeds without gear shifting. The drive has\nan odometer pickup (measuring distance traveled) that sends\ndata to the navigation system. Each motor develops 0.18\nkilowatt (I/$-horsepower) and operates from a 36-volt input.\nEach wheel has a mechanical brake connected to the hand\ncontroller. Moving the controller rearward de-energizes the\ndrive motors and forces brake shoes against a drum, stopping\nwheel hub rotation. Full rear movement of the controller\nengages and locks a parking brake.\nThe chassis is suspended from each wheel by two parallel\narms mounted on torsion bars and connected to each traction\ndrive. Tire deflection allows a 35.6-centimeter (14-inch)\nground clearance when the vehicle is fully loaded, and 43.2\ncentimeters (17 inches) when unloaded.\n\n~ o t h r o n t and r e a r wheels have independent s t e e r i n g\nf\nsystems t h a t allow a "wall-to-wall" t u r n i n g r a d i u s of 3.1\nmeters ( 1 2 2 i n c h e s ) , e x a c t l y t h e v e h i c l e length. I f e i t h e r\nset of wheels has a s t e e r i n g f a i l u r e , i t s s t e e r i n g system\ncan be disengaged and t h e t r a v e r s e can continue with t h e\na c t i v e s t e e r i n g assembly. Each wheel can a l s o be manually\nuncoupled from t h e t r a c t i o n d r i v e and brake t o allow " f r e e\nwheeling" about t h e d r i v e housing.\nPushing t h e hand c o n t r o l l e r forward increased forward\nspeed; r e a r movement reduces speed. Forward and r e v e r s e are\nc o n t r o l l e d by a knob on t h e c o n t r o l l e r \' s v e r t i c a l s t e m . With\nt h e knob pushed down, t h e c o n t r o l l e r can only be pivoted f o r ward; with it pushed up, t h e c o n t r o l l e r can be pivoted t o t h e\nrear f o r reverse.\nCrew S t a t i o n\nThe crew s t a t i o n c o n s i s t s of t h e c o n t r o l and d i s p l a y\nconsole, s e a t s , seat b e l t s , an armrest, f o o t r e s t s , inboard\nand outboard handholds, toeholds, f l o o r p a n e l s , and fenders.\nThe c o n t r o l and d i s p l a y console i s s e p a r a t e d i n t o two\nmain p a r t s : The t o p p b r t i o n holds navigation system d i s p l a y s ;\nt h e lower p o r t i o n c o n t a i n s monitors and c o n t r o l s . They a r e folded f l a t during launch and erected by crewmen a f t e r deployment. The s e a t backs support t h e\na s t r o n a u t p o r t a b l e l i f e support systems. The\nLCRU i s manufactured by RCA.\nLRV,\n\n-78-\n\nTELEVISION AND GROUND\nCOMMANDED TELEVISION ASSEMBLY\nTwo different color television cameras will be used\nduring the Apollo 17 mission. One, manufactured by Westinghouse, will be used in the command module. It will be fitted\nwith a 5-centimeter (2-inch) black and white monitor to aid\nthe crew in focus and exposure adjustment.\n\n\'\n\nThe other camera, manufactured by RCA, is for lunar\nsurface use and will be operated from the lunar roving vehicle\n(LRV) with signal transmission through the lunar communication\nrelay unit rather than through the LM communications system.\nWhile on the LRV, the camera will be mounted on the\nground commanded television assembly (GCTA). The camera can\nbe aimed and controlled by astronauts or it can be remotely\ncontrolled by personnel located in the Mission Control Center. Remote command capability includes camera "on" and\n"off", pan, tilt, zoom, iris open/closed (\xc2\xa32.2 to \xc2\xa322) and\npeak or average automatic light control.\nThe GCTA is capable of tilting the TV camera upward 85\ndegrees, downward 45 degrees, and panning the camera 350\ndegrees between mechanical stops. Pan and tilt rates are\napproximately 3 degrees per second.\nThe TV lens can be zoomed from a focal length of 12.5mm\nto 75mm corresponding to a field of view from 9 to 54 degrees.\nAt the end of the third EVA, the crew will park the LRV\nabout 91.4 m (300 ft.) east of the LM so that the color TV\ncamera can cover the LM ascent from the lunar surface. Because of a time delay in a signal going the quarter million\nmiles out to the Moon, Mission Control must anticipate ascent\nengine ignition by about two seconds with the tilt command.\nThe GCTA and camera each weigh approximately 5.9 kg\n13 1 .\nThe overall length of the camera is 46 cm (18.0 in. )\nits width is 17 cm (6.7 in.), and its height is 25 cm (10 i . ,\nn)\nIt is powered from the LCRU battery supply, or externally\nfrom the LRV batteries. The GCTA is built by RCA.\n\n.\n\n-79-\n\nPHOTOGRAPHIC EOUIPMENT\n\nS t i l l and motion p i c t u r e s w i l l be made of most spacec r a f t maneuvers and crew l u n a r s u r f a c e a c t i v i t i e s . Included i n t h e l a r g e r medical a c c e s s o r i e s k i t\nare a n t i b i o t i c ointment, s k i n cream, eye drops, nose drops,\ns p a r e biomedical h a r n e s s e s , o r a l thermometer and p i l l s of\nt h e following types: 1 8 p a i n , 1 2 s t i m u l a n t , 1 2 motion s i c k n e s s , 4 8 d i a r r h e a , 6 0 decongestant, 2 1 s l e e p i n g , 72 a s p i r i n\nand 60 each of two t y p e s o f a n t i b i o t i c . A s m a l l e r command\nmodule a u x i l i a r y drug k i t c o n t a i n s 80 and 1 2 of two t y p e s o f\np i l l s f o r t r e a t m e n t of c a r d i a c arrythymia and two i n j e c t o r s\nf o r t h e same symptom.\nThe l u n a r module medical k i t c o n t a i n s eye drops, nose\ndrops, a n t i b i o t i c ointment, bandages and t h e following p i l l s :\n4 s t i m u l a n t , 4 p a i n , 8 decongestant, 1 2 d i a r r h e a , 1 2 a s p i r i n\nand 6 s l e e p i n g . A s m a l l e r k i t i n t h e LM c o n t a i n s 8 and 4\nu n i t s of i n j e c t a b l e drugs f o r c a r d i a c arrythymia and 2 u n i t s\nf o r p a i n suppression.\n\nCrew Food System\nThe Apollo 17 crew selected menus for their flight\nfrom the largest variety of foods ever available for a\nU.S. manned mission. As on Apollo 16, the preflight, inflight, and postflight diets are being monitored to facilitate interpretation of the medical tests.\nMenus were designed upon individual crewmember physiological requirements in the unique conditions of weightlessness and one-sixth gravity on the lunar surface. Daily\nmenus provide approximately 2500 calories per day for each\ncrewmember.\nFood items are assembled into meal units and identified as to crewmember and sequence of consumption. Foods\nstored in the "pantry1\'\nmay be used as substitutions for\nnominal meal items so long as the nutrient intake for a\n24-hour period is not altered significantly.\nThere are various types of food used in the menus.\nThese include freeze-dried rehydratables in spoon-bowl\npackages; thermostabilized foods (wet packs) in flexible\npackages and metal easy-open cans; intermediate moisture\nfoods; dry bite-size cubes; and beverages.\nWater for drinking and rehydrating food is obtained\nfrom two sources in the Command Module -- a portable dispenser for drinking water and a water spigot at the food\npreparation station which supplies water at about 145\ndegrees and 55 degrees Fahrenheit. The potable water\ndispenser provides a continuous flow of water as long as\nthe trigger is held down, while the food preparation\nspigot dispenses water in one-ounce increments.\nA continuous-flow water dispenser similar to the one\nin the Command Module is used aboard the Lunar Module for\ncold water reconstitution of food stowed aboard the Lunar\nModule.\nWater is injected into a food package and the package\nis kneaded and allowed to sit for several minutes. The\nbag top is then cut to open and the food eaten with a spoon.\nAfter a meal, germicide tablets are placed in each bag to\nprevent fermentation oZ any residual food and gas formation.\nThe bags are then rolled and stowed in waste disposal areas\nin the spacecraft.\n\nAn improved Skylab beverage package design will be used\nby the crew to measure water consumption. Functional aspects\nof the package and the behavior of liquid during extended\nperiods of weightlessness will be observed.\nThe in-suit drink device will contain water as on the\nApollo 15 mission. As on Apollo 15 and 16, the crewmen on\nthe lunar surface will have the option tossnack on an insuit food bar.\nThe nutritionally complete fruitcake provides all the\nnutrients needed by man in their correct proportions. The\nfruitcake contains many ingredients such as: soy flour,\nwheat flour, sugar, eggs, salt, cherries, pineapple, nuts,\nraisins, and shortening. Vitamins have been added. The\nproduct is heat sterilized in an impermeable flexible pouch\nand is shelf-stable until opened. This fruitcake can provZde\na nutritious snack or meal. This food is planned for use\nin the future in the Space Shuttle program as a contingency\nfood system.\nThe irradiated ham provides the crew with a shelf-stable\nslice of ham 12 mm thick. Each slice weighs about 100 grams\nand may be used for making sandwiches during flight. The\nradiation sterilization (radappertization) is performed while\nThe absorbed irradiation dose is 3.7\nthe ham is at - 4 0 \xc2\xb0 C .\nto 4.3 million rads. This gives an excellent product with\nan expected shelf-life of 3 years.\nThe fruitcake and ham slices were specially developed\nand provided for Apollo 17 by the U.S. Army Natick Laboratories,\nNatick, Massachusetts.\nNew foods for the Apollo 17 mission are irradiated\nsterilized ham, nutrient complete fruitcake, and rehydratable\ntea and lemonade beverages.\n\nAPOLLO 1 7 C M MENU Eugene A. H s e r v e d as a member\nAA\ne\no f t h e a s t r o n a u t s u p p o r t crew f o r t h e Apollo X f l i g h t\nand a s backup l u n a r module p i l o t f o r t h e Apollo X I 1 1\nflight.\nH s e r v e d a s l u n a r module p i l o t f o r t h e Apollo 16\ne\nmission.\n\n.\n\nSPACEFLIGHT TRACKING AND DATA SUPPORT NETWORK\nNASA\'s worldwide Spaceflight Tracking and Data Network\n(STDN) will provide communication with the Apollo astronauts,\ntheir launch vehicle and spacecraft. It will also maintain\nthe communications link between Earth and the Apollo experiments left on the lunar surface by earlier Apallo crews.\nThe STDN is linked together by the NASA Communication\nNetwork (NASCOM) which provides for all information and data\nflow.\nIn support of Apollo 17, the STDN will employ 11 ground\ntracking stations equipped with 9.1-meter (30-foot) and 25.9 m\n(85-ft) antennas, and instrumented tracking ship, and four\ninstrumented aircraft. This portion of the STDN was known\nformerly as the Manned Space Flight Network. For Apollo 17,\nthe network will be augmented by the 64-m (210-ft.) antenna\nsystem at Goldstone, Calif. (a unit of NASA\'s Deep Space\nNetwrok), and if required the 64-m (210-ft.) radio antenna of\nthe National Radio Astronomy Observatory at Parkes, Australia.\nThe STDN is maintained and operated by the NASA Goddard\nSpace Flight Center, Greenbelt, Md., under the direction of\nNASA\'s Office of Tracking and Data Acquisition. Goddard will\nbecome an emergency control center if the Houston Mission\nControl Center is impaired for an extended time.\nNASA Communications Network (NASCOM). The tracking network\nis linked together by the NASA Communications Network. A11\ninformation flows to and from Mission Control Center, (MCC),\nHouston, and the Apollo spacecraft over this communications\nsystem.\nThe NASCOM consists of more than 3.2 million circuit\nkilometers (1.7 million nautical miles), using satellites,\nsubmarine cables, land lines, microwave systems, and high\nfrequency radio facilities. NASCOM control center is located\nat Goddard. Regional communication switching centers are\nin Madrid; Canberra, Australia; Honolulu; and Guam.\nIntelsat communications satellites will be used for\nApollo 17. One satellite over the Atlantic will link\nGoddard with Ascension Island and the Vanguard tracking\nship. Another Atlantic satellite will provide a direct\nlink between Madrid and Goddard for TV signals received from\nthe spacecraft. One satellite positioned over the mid-Pacific\nwill link Carnarvon, Australia; Canberra, Guam and Hawaii\nwith Goddard through the Jamesburg, California ground\nstation. An alternate route of communications between Spain\nand Australia is available through another Intelsat satellite\npositioned over the Indian Ocean if required.\n\nMission Operations: Prelaunch tests, liftoff, and\nEarth orbital flight of the Apollo 17 are supported by the\nApollo subnet station at Merritt Island, Fla., 6.4 km\n(3,5 nm) from the launch pad.\nDuring the critical\nthe Apollo 17 into Earth\ntracking, telemetry, and\nsingle sea-going station\nr\nstationed about 1,610 kn\n\nperiod of launch and insertion of\norbit, the USNS Vanguard provides\ncommunications functions. This\nof the Apollo subnet will be\n(870 nm) southeast of Bermuda.\n\nWhen the Apollo 17 conducts the translunar injection\n(TLI) Earth orbit for the Moon, two Apollo range instrumentation aircraft (ARIA) will record telemetry data from Apollo\nand relay voice communications between the astronauts and the\nMCC at Houston. These aircraft will be airborne between\nSouth America and the west coast of Africa. ARIA 1 will cover\nTLI ignition and ARIA 2 will monitor TLI burn completion.\nApproximately 1 hour after the spacecraft has been injected into a translunar trajectory, three prime MSFN stations\nwill take over tracking and communication with Apollo.\nThese stations are equipped with 25.9 m (85-ft.1 antennas.\nEach of the prime stations, located at Goldstone,\nMadrid, and Honeysuckle, Australia is equipped with dual\nsystems for tracking the command module in lunar orbit and\nthe lunar module in separate flight paths or at rest on the\nMoon.\nFor reentry, two ARIA (Apollo Range Instrumented Aircraft)\nwill be deployed to the landing area to relay communications\nbetween Apollo and Mission Control at Houston. These aircraft also will provide position information on the Apollo\nafter the blackout phase of reentry has passed.\nAn applications technology satellite (ATS) terminal has\nbeen placed aboard the recovery ship USS Ticonderoga to relay\ncommand control communications of the recovery forces, via\nNASA\'s ATS satellite. Communications will be relayed from\nthe deck-mounted terminal to the NASA tracking stations at\nMojave, Calif, and Rosman, N.C., through Goddard to the recovery control centers located in Hawaii and Houston.\n\nPrior to recovery, the astronauts aeromedical records\nare transmitted via the ATS satellite to the recovery ship\nfor comparison with the physical data obtained in the postflight examination performed aboard the recovery ship,\n\nTelevision Transmissions: Television from the Apollo\nspacecraft during the journey to and from the Moon and on the\nlunar surface will be received by the three prime stations,\naugmented by the 64-m (210-ft.) antennas at Goldstone and\nParkes. The color TV signal must be converted at MSC, Houston.\nA black and white version of the color signal can be released\nlocally from the stations in Spain and Australia.\nWhile the camera is mounted on the lunar roving vehicle\n(LRV), the TV signals will be transmitted directly to tracking stations as the astronauts explore the Moon.\nOnce the LRV has been parked near the lunar module, its\nbatteries will have about 80 hours of operating life. This\nwill allow ground controllers to position the camera for\nviewing the lunar module liftoff, post liftoff geology, and\nother scenes.\n\nENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT OF APOLLOISATURN V MISSION\nStudies of NASA space mission operations have concluded\nthat ApolLo does not significantly effect the human environment in the areas of air, water, noise or nuclear radiation.\nDuring the launch of the Apollo/Saturn V space vehicle,\nproducts exhausted from Saturn first stage engines in all\ncases are within an ample margin of safety. At lower altitudes, where toxicity is of concern, the carbon monoxide is\noxidized to carbon dioxide upon exposure at its high temperature to the surrounding air. The quantities released are\ntwo or more orders of magnitude below the recognized levels\nfor concern in regard to significant modification of the\nenvironment. The second and third stage main propulsion\nsystems generate only water and a small amount of hydrogen.\nSolid propellant ullage and retro rocket products are released\nand rapidly dispersed in the upper atmosphere at altitudes\nabove 70 kilometers (43.5 miles). This material will effectively\nnever reach sea level and, consequently, poses no toxicity\nhazard.\nShould an abort after launch be necessary, some RP-1\nfuel (kerosene) could reach the ocean. However, toxicity of\nRP-1 is slight and impact on marine life and waterfowl are\nconsidered negligible due to its dispersive characteristics.\nCalculations of dumping an aborted S-IC stage into the ocean\nshowed that spreading and evaporating of the fuel occurred in\none to four hours.\nThere are only two times during a nominal Apollo mission\nwhen above normal overall sound pressure levels are encountered.\nThese two times are during vehicle boost from the launch pad\nand the sonic boom experienced when the spacecraft enters the\nEarth\'s atmosphere. Sonic boom is not a significant nuisance\nsince it occurs over the mid-Pacific Ocean.\nNASA and the Department of Defense have made a comprehensive\nstudy of noise levels and other hazards to be encountered for\nlaunching vehicles of the Saturn V magnitude. For uncontrolled\nareas the overall sound pressure levels are well below those\nwhich cause damage or discomfort. Saturn launches have had no\ndeleterious effects on wildlife which has actually increased\nin the NASA-protected areas of Merritt Island.\n\nA source of potential radiation hazard but highly unlikely, is the fuel capsule of the radioisotope thermoelectric\ngenerator supplied by the Atomic Bnergy Commission which\nprovides electric power for Apollo lunar surface experiments.\nThe fuel cask is designed to contain the nuclear fuel during\nnormal operations and in the event of aborts so that the\npossibility of radiation contamination is negligible. Extensive\nsafety analvses and tests have been conducted which demonstrated\nthat the fuel would be safely contained under almost a11 credible accident conditions.\n\nP O R M M N GMN\nRGA\nAAE ET\nThe Apollo Program i s t h e r e s p o n s i b i l i t y o f t h e O f f i c e\nof Manned Space F l i g h t (OMSF), N a t i o n a l Aeronautics and\nSpace A d m i n i s t r a t i o n , Washington, D.
